#### E.2: Draft Contract Language

**Representation & Warranty (Article 3 – Seller Representations):**

```markdown
**Section 3.18: Recycling Operations**

(a) **MRF Facilities and Capacity.** The Company operates eight (8) material recovery facilities ("MRFs") located in Arizona (3), Nevada (3), and New Mexico (2) with aggregate nameplate processing capacity of 850,000 tons per year. Schedule 3.18(a) sets forth for each MRF: (i) facility address and square footage; (ii) processing capacity (tons/year); (iii) FY2024 actual throughput (tons/year); (iv) major equipment inventory with installation dates; (v) current operator certifications and permits; and (vi) capital expenditures for FY2022, FY2023, and FY2024.

(b) **Financial Performance.** The Company's recycling segment generated the following financial results for FY2024: (i) revenue of $240M; (ii) direct operating costs (excluding depreciation) of $269M; (iii) EBITDA of negative $29M; and (iv) EBITDA margin of negative 12%. Schedule 3.18(b) sets forth monthly revenue, cost, throughput, and commodity realization data for each of the eight MRFs for the 36-month period ending December 31, 2024.

(c) **Contamination Rates.** The Company's weighted average contamination rate across all MRFs for FY2024 was 22.0%, as determined by third-party contamination audits conducted monthly by RRS (Resource Recycling Systems) or other independent auditors. "Contamination Rate" means the percentage by weight of Inbound Recyclables that cannot be processed into Saleable Commodities due to the presence of non-recyclable materials, calculated using the methodology set forth in Schedule 3.18(c)-1. Schedule 3.18(c)-2 sets forth monthly contamination rates by MRF for the 36-month period ending December 31, 2024, including: (i) contamination audit reports; (ii) residue disposal tonnage and costs; and (iii) contamination-related liquidated damages assessed or cure notices received.

(d) **Technology and Equipment Condition.** Schedule 3.18(d) sets forth a complete inventory of optical sorters, robotic picking systems, AI-based contamination detection systems, and quality control infrastructure deployed at each MRF, including: (i) equipment type, manufacturer, and model; (ii) installation date and expected useful life; (iii) current operating status (operational, non-operational, or degraded performance); (iv) maintenance capital expenditure requirements for the succeeding 24 months; and (v) technology gap analysis comparing Company systems to industry-standard deployment levels as of January 1, 2025.

(e) **Municipal Contract Contamination Compliance.** Except as set forth on Schedule 3.18(e), the Company is in compliance with all contamination rate thresholds, quality standards, and performance requirements under its municipal recycling contracts. Schedule 3.18(e) identifies all instances during the 36-month period ending December 31, 2024 where: (i) contamination rates exceeded contractual thresholds; (ii) liquidated damages were assessed or reserved; (iii) cure notices were received; (iv) corrective action plans were required; or (v) contract termination was threatened or discussed by the municipal counterparty.

(f) **Commodity Markets and Pricing.** The Company's blended commodity revenue per ton for FY2024 was $59/ton, calculated as total commodity sales revenue divided by total saleable commodity tonnage. Schedule 3.18(f) sets forth monthly commodity pricing by category (mixed paper, sorted plastics #1 PET, sorted plastics #2 HDPE, aluminum, steel) and end-market buyer for each MRF for the 36-month period ending December 31, 2024. The Company has no commodity price hedges, forward sales contracts, or other arrangements fixing commodity prices for periods extending beyond 90 days as of the Closing Date.

(g) **Capital Investment Plans.** Schedule 3.18(g) sets forth the Company's board-approved capital investment plans for recycling operations for calendar years 2025, 2026, and 2027, including: (i) planned technology investments by MRF with estimated costs and implementation timelines; (ii) contamination rate improvement targets; (iii) expected EBITDA margin improvement by year; and (iv) financing sources for planned investments.

(h) **No Material Adverse Developments.** Since December 31, 2024, there has been no Material Adverse Effect on the recycling segment, including: (i) no municipal contract has been terminated or non-renewed; (ii) no contamination rates have exceeded 25% at any MRF for two consecutive months; (iii) no major equipment failures have reduced processing capacity by more than 15% at any MRF; and (iv) commodity prices have not declined more than 20% from December 31, 2024 levels in any major commodity category.
```

**Indemnification Provision (Article 8 – Indemnification):**

```markdown
**Section 8.2: Seller Indemnification**

(c) **Recycling-Specific Indemnification.** In addition to the general indemnification obligations under Section 8.2(a), Seller shall indemnify, defend, and hold harmless Buyer and its Affiliates from and against any Losses arising out of, resulting from, or related to:

(i) **Contamination Performance Failures:** Any breach of Section 3.18(e) (Municipal Contract Contamination Compliance), including: (A) liquidated damages assessed by municipal counterparties for contamination rates exceeding contractual thresholds during the 36-month period ending at Closing; (B) costs incurred to implement corrective action plans required by municipal counterparties; (C) revenue losses from contract terminations or non-renewals based in whole or in part on contamination rate performance; and (D) increased residue disposal costs arising from contamination rates exceeding the Company's historical 22% weighted average by more than 10% (i.e., contamination >24.2%) during the 36-month period following Closing.

(ii) **Technology Condition Misrepresentations:** Any inaccuracy in Schedule 3.18(d) (Technology and Equipment Condition) resulting in: (A) capital expenditures exceeding the amounts identified in Schedule 3.18(d)(iv) by more than 25%; (B) equipment failures requiring unscheduled replacement within 12 months of Closing; or (C) performance degradation preventing achievement of contamination rate improvement targets identified in Schedule 3.18(g).

(iii) **Operating Loss Exposure:** To the extent that recycling segment EBITDA for any of the first three full fiscal years following Closing is more negative than negative $29M (as adjusted for changes in throughput volume using a marginal EBITDA assumption of -$15/ton), Seller shall pay to Buyer the amount of such excess losses, calculated annually and payable within 90 days after Buyer's delivery of audited annual financial statements. **Example:** If Year 1 post-Closing recycling EBITDA is -$42M (on throughput equal to FY2024 levels), Seller owes $13M ($42M - $29M). This indemnity is **uncapped** and survives for three years following Closing.

(iv) **Survival and Limitations:** The indemnification obligations under this Section 8.2(c) survive for **three (3) years** following the Closing Date, notwithstanding the general 18-month survival period in Section 8.6. The indemnification obligations under Section 8.2(c)(iii) (Operating Loss Exposure) are **not subject to the $50M cap** or **$5M basket** applicable to general indemnification claims under Section 8.4.
```

**Escrow Agreement Terms (Article 2 – Purchase Price and Payment):**

```markdown
**Section 2.3: Escrow Arrangement**

(c) **Recycling Performance Escrow.** At Closing, Buyer shall deposit into escrow with [Escrow Agent] (the "Recycling Escrow") an amount equal to **$150,000,000**, which shall be held and released in accordance with the Escrow Agreement attached as Exhibit B. The Recycling Escrow shall secure Seller's indemnification obligations under Section 8.2(c) and shall be released as follows:

(i) **Year 1 Release:** $50,000,000 shall be released to Seller on the first anniversary of the Closing Date if: (A) no municipal contracts have been terminated or non-renewed based on contamination performance; (B) no Events of Default have occurred under the Credit Agreement related to recycling operational performance; and (C) recycling segment EBITDA for Year 1 is equal to or better than negative $29M (as adjusted for throughput).

(ii) **Year 2 Release:** $50,000,000 shall be released to Seller on the second anniversary of the Closing Date if the conditions in Section 2.3(c)(i) remain satisfied and: (A) weighted average contamination rates have improved to 20% or below across all MRFs; and (B) at least $25M of technology investment (as defined in Schedule 3.18(g)) has been completed.

(iii) **Year 3 Release:** $50,000,000 shall be released to Seller on the third anniversary of the Closing Date if the conditions in Sections 2.3(c)(i) and 2.3(c)(ii) remain satisfied and: (A) weighted average contamination rates have improved to 17% or below; and (B) recycling segment EBITDA has improved to negative $15M or better.

(iv) **Offset Rights:** To the extent Buyer has indemnification claims under Section 8.2(c) that have been finally determined (whether by agreement, arbitration, or litigation), such amounts shall be offset against scheduled Escrow releases, with any excess claim amounts payable by Seller within 30 days.

(v) **Interest:** The Escrow Account shall earn interest at the Federal Funds Rate, with interest allocated pro rata between Buyer and Seller based on the proportion of Escrow funds ultimately released to each party.
```

**Pre-Closing Covenant (Article 6 – Covenants):**

```markdown
**Section 6.5: Recycling Technology Investment Plan**

(a) **Preparation of Investment Plan.** Within 45 days following execution of this Agreement, Seller shall cause the Company to prepare and deliver to Buyer a comprehensive Recycling Technology Investment Plan (the "Investment Plan") that includes:

(i) **Facility-Specific Analysis:** For each of the eight MRFs, a detailed assessment of: (A) current contamination rate performance and root causes; (B) existing technology infrastructure and identified gaps relative to industry-standard deployment (optical sorters, AI detection systems, robotic picking, QC infrastructure); (C) recommended technology investments with vendor specifications, budgeted costs, and implementation timelines; and (D) projected contamination rate improvements and EBITDA impact by year (Years 1-3 post-Closing).

(ii) **Aggregate Investment Plan:** A consolidated plan identifying: (A) total capital investment required across all eight MRFs; (B) phased deployment schedule (identifying which facilities receive investments in Year 1, Year 2, and Year 3); (C) financing sources (operating cash flow, debt capacity, or equity contribution); (D) key execution risks and mitigation strategies; and (E) expected return on investment (payback period, IRR, NPV at 8% WACC).

(iii) **Municipal Contract Impact Assessment:** Analysis of how contamination improvements will affect municipal contract renewal probability and RFP competitiveness, specifically for: (A) Albuquerque contract (expires 2026); (B) Tucson contract (expires 2027); (C) Henderson contract (expires 2028); and (D) Phoenix franchise RFP (2027).

(b) **Buyer Review and Approval.** Buyer shall have 30 days to review the Investment Plan and provide written comments. Seller and Buyer shall negotiate in good faith to reach agreement on a Final Investment Plan that demonstrates: (i) contamination rate improvement to 15% or below within 24 months of Closing; (ii) recycling segment EBITDA improvement to breakeven or better within 36 months of Closing; and (iii) total capital investment not exceeding $70M.

(c) **Binding Implementation Commitment.** The Final Investment Plan, once approved by Buyer, shall be attached as **Schedule 6.5** and shall constitute a binding obligation of the Company post-Closing. The Company's failure to substantially implement the Final Investment Plan (defined as achieving at least 75% of planned capital expenditures and 70% of contamination improvement targets by the timelines specified therein) shall constitute an Event of Default under the Credit Agreement and a breach of Seller's representations under Section 3.18(g).
```

#### E.3: Pre-Closing Conditions (Article 7 – Conditions to Closing)

| Closing Condition | Type | Description | Rationale |
|------------------|------|-------------|-----------|
| **Technology Investment Plan Approval** | Buyer Condition (§7.2(c)) | Buyer and Seller have agreed upon Final Investment Plan per Section 6.5(b), and Company's board has approved funding commitment | Ensures enforceable post-Closing roadmap for contamination remediation; protects Phoenix RFP competitiveness |
| **Municipal Contract Cure Notices Resolved** | Buyer Condition (§7.2(d)) | All outstanding contamination-based cure notices (Albuquerque, Tucson, Henderson) have been withdrawn or extended ≥12 months | Eliminates immediate contract termination risk during Year 1 integration; provides runway for technology implementation |
| **Third-Party Technology Assessment** | Buyer Condition (§7.2(e)) | Independent consultant (Crowe LLP or equivalent) has issued report confirming: (i) $40M-$64M capex range reasonable; (ii) 18-24 month implementation timeline achievable; (iii) 22% → 15% contamination improvement feasible | Independent validation of remediation feasibility; supports lender underwriting for post-Closing capex covenant compliance |
| **No Material Recycling Deterioration** | Buyer Condition (§7.2(f)) | Since signing, no MRF contamination rate has exceeded 27% for two consecutive months; no additional municipal contract termination threats received | Backstop against further operational deterioration during signing-to-closing period (estimated 4-6 months) |

#### E.4: Counter-Party Response Anticipation and Negotiation Strategy

| Issue | Seller's Likely Position | Seller's Arguments | Our Counter-Arguments | Negotiation Strategy |
|-------|------------------------|-------------------|---------------------|---------------------|
| **Escrow Amount ($150M)** | Excessive; proposes $50M | (1) WM/Advanced Disposal escrow was 5.8% of purchase price; $150M represents 4.2%, already below precedent; (2) recycling losses are "known"; buyer should adjust purchase price, not hold escrow; (3) $150M ties up seller's liquidity for 3 years | (1) WM/Advanced had $180M escrow (5.8%) for $42M annual losses = 4.3x loss multiple; our $150M for $29M annual losses = 5.2x multiple, **consistent with precedent**;⁹⁵⁹ (2) escrow protects against contract termination **contingent risk** ($40.3M PV), not just operating losses (which should reduce purchase price); (3) graduated release structure (Year 1: $50M, Year 2: $50M, Year 3: $50M) provides liquidity if performance improves | **Opening:** $150M escrow with graduated release tied to contamination improvement milestones (22% → 20% → 17% over 3 years). **Target:** $110M-$125M escrow if seller agrees to uncapped recycling-specific indemnity under Section 8.2(c)(iii). **Walk-Away:** Minimum $75M escrow covering 18-month contract termination runway; below this, increase purchase price reduction from $100M to $175M to compensate for reduced protection. |
| **Uncapped Operating Loss Indemnity (§8.2(c)(iii))** | Absolutely opposed; proposes $25M cap | (1) Buyer has superior information and control post-Closing; seller should not bear unlimited risk for buyer's operational decisions; (2) technology investment is buyer's choice—seller shouldn't indemnify buyer's execution failures; (3) uncapped indemnity creates unlimited liability, unmarketable to seller's lenders/investors | (1) Seller controls pre-Closing investment decisions—deferring MRF modernization created this exposure; uncapped indemnity for 3 years simply allocates seller's historical under-investment risk;⁹⁶⁰ (2) caps are appropriate for unknown risks (e.g., environmental liabilities from past practices), but recycling underperformance is **quantified and ongoing**—seller has actual knowledge of -$29M annual EBITDA; (3) WM/Advanced included **uncapped** recycling indemnity for commodity-linked losses despite buyer control argument⁹⁶¹ | **Opening:** Uncapped indemnity for 3 years, calculated as actual recycling EBITDA vs. -$29M baseline (adjusted for throughput). **Target:** $75M cap if combined with $150M escrow (total protection $225M covers P90 scenario); alternatively, uncapped for Year 1-2, $50M cap for Year 3. **Walk-Away:** Seller must accept either (a) uncapped indemnity OR (b) $150M+ escrow—cannot reject both protections given magnitude of quantified exposure. |
| **Purchase Price Adjustment** | Objects to $100M+ reduction; offers $35M | (1) EPR legislation will pass in AZ/NV by 2027-2028, fundamentally changing economics (cites California PRO payments of $185/ton);⁹⁶² (2) commodity prices are cyclical—current $35/ton mixed paper depressed, historical range $80-$120/ton suggests mean reversion;⁹⁶³ (3) technology investment has 72% IRR—buyer will profit from modernization, shouldn't get purchase price reduction too | (1) AZ HB 2234 died in committee March 2025; NV SB 374 tabled; legislative probability <25% within contract terms, and even if passed, 3-5 year implementation timeline means losses continue;⁹⁶⁴ (2) China National Sword remains in effect with no reversal signals; U.S. domestic end-use markets cannot absorb contaminated recyclables at pre-2018 prices;⁹⁶⁵ (3) buyer's potential upside from technology investment is speculative and execution-risk-laden; seller's 5-8 year underinvestment created certain losses that should reduce purchase price **now**; WM/Advanced precedent: $425M reduction (12% discount) for $42M annual losses = **10.1x loss multiple**; applying to UESC: $29M × 10.0x = $290M justified reduction⁹⁶⁶ | **Opening:** $290M purchase price reduction (10.0x annual loss multiple per WM/Advanced precedent). **Target:** $150M-$200M reduction if seller accepts: (a) $150M escrow; (b) uncapped operating loss indemnity for 3 years; and (c) binding technology investment plan as closing condition. **Trade-Off Matrix:** Seller can choose: (1) High protection ($290M reduction + $75M escrow + capped indemnity); (2) Medium protection ($175M reduction + $125M escrow + capped $75M indemnity); (3) Lower price reduction ($100M) ONLY if seller accepts uncapped indemnity + $150M escrow + tech investment plan closing condition. **Walk-Away:** Minimum $100M reduction given quantified -$115.8M PV operating losses; any lower and transaction NPV turns negative for buyer. |
| **Technology Investment as Closing Condition** | Opposes; argues premature | (1) Technology plan requires 6-12 months to develop properly; impossible to finalize pre-Closing; (2) buyer will control Company post-Closing and can implement any technology strategy; condition is unnecessary; (3) creates deal risk—if buyer unreasonably withholds approval of investment plan, seller cannot close | (1) 45-day preparation period (Section 6.5(a)) + 30-day buyer review (Section 6.5(b)) = 75 days total, achievable within typical 4-6 month signing-to-closing timeline for $3.6B transactions;⁹⁶⁷ (2) **Phoenix RFP competitiveness depends on credible contamination remediation commitment**—without board-approved, funded technology plan, RFP submittal (Q4 2026) will be uncompetitive, jeopardizing $320M annual revenue franchise (see Section III.B);⁹⁶⁸ (3) condition includes good-faith negotiation requirement and objective parameters ($70M capex cap, 15% contamination target, 24-month timeline)—buyer cannot unreasonably withhold approval; if parties cannot agree, either can terminate and pay $50M reverse break fee under Section 9.3(b) | **Opening:** Closing condition requiring Final Investment Plan approval per Section 6.5(b) before Closing. **Target:** If seller strongly opposes, convert to "commercially reasonable efforts" covenant: Seller shall use commercially reasonable efforts to deliver Investment Plan within 60 days of signing, and if delivered, buyer commits to $52M funding allocation in Year 1-2 capital budget. **Walk-Away:** Seller must agree to **either** (a) binding Investment Plan closing condition OR (b) post-Closing performance escrow ($50M held for 18 months, released only if contamination improves to 19% by Month 18)—buyer will not proceed without enforceable contamination improvement mechanism given Phoenix RFP dependency. |

A fixed-price contract is an explicit assignment of the risk of market price increases to the seller and the risk of market price decreases to the buyer."); *Missouri Public Service Co. v. Peabody Coal Co.*, 583 S.W.2d 721, 727 (Mo. Ct. App. 1979) (rejecting impracticability defense despite 500% coal price increase because "the possibility of market price fluctuation is the very risk the parties address in a fixed price contract").

Annual revenue impact: $19/ton × 705,000 tons × 0.58 = $7.8M, plus residue cost $3.7M, plus lost commodity volume (contamination destroys saleable tonnage) $1.9M = $13.4M total annual revenue shortfall attributable to contamination performance gap [MEDIUM CONFIDENCE - calculation based on company and peer disclosures].

⁹⁰⁵ Resource Recycling, *Historical Commodity Price Data 2010-2024* (showing mixed paper pricing: 2010-2011 range $80-$95/ton, 2012-2014 range $95-$120/ton during China commodity import boom, 2015-2017 decline to $75-$95/ton, 2018 National Sword crash to $35-$50/ton, 2019-2024 stabilization at $30-$40/ton) [HIGH CONFIDENCE - verified industry pricing data].

⁹⁰⁷ Waste Management commodity realization $78/ton (FY2023 Investor Presentation) vs. UESC $59/ton (FY2024 actual) = $19/ton quality discount attributable to contamination-driven commodity devaluation; this quality discount persists across commodity price cycles because contaminated recyclables receive discounted pricing regardless of absolute market levels [HIGH CONFIDENCE - peer comparison from public disclosures].

⁹⁰⁸ UESC municipal contract review (Tucson Agreement § 3.2, Henderson Agreement § 4.1, Albuquerque Agreement § 2.3) shows fixed monthly service fees: Tucson $1.95M/month ($23.4M annually), Henderson $1.83M/month ($22.0M annually), Albuquerque $1.25M/month ($15.0M annually). No commodity price adjustment clauses exist in any of the three contracts—contractor retains 100% of commodity revenue but also bears 100% of commodity price risk [HIGH CONFIDENCE - contract language review].

⁹³⁶ Consolidated EBITDA compression scenario: FY2024 baseline $858M less recycling ongoing losses $29M annually less Teamsters pattern bargaining Year 2-4 average impact $28M annually = $801M, assuming flat baseline growth in collection/disposal segments (conservative assumption given 3.2% industry growth rate). EBITDA margin: $801M ÷ $3,140M projected revenue (FY2024 $3,145M less recycling headwinds) = 25.5% margin, down from 27.3% baseline, ranking UESC below all major public peers and approaching distressed territory (margins <25% typically trigger lender concern and analyst downgrades in waste industry) [MEDIUM CONFIDENCE - scenario modeling with conservative growth assumptions].

⁹³⁷ Fixed Charge Coverage Ratio covenant calculation (Credit Agreement § 7.11(b)): Consolidated EBITDA ÷ (Consolidated Interest Expense + Consolidated Scheduled Debt Amortization) ≥ 1.20x required minimum. FY2024 actual: $858M EBITDA ÷ ($245M interest + $425M scheduled amortization) = 1.28x, providing 0.08x (6.7%) cushion above 1.20x minimum. Each $10M EBITDA reduction decreases ratio by 0.015x; $57M combined recycling/Teamsters impact reduces ratio by 0.086x to 1.194x, violating 1.20x minimum. Senior Secured Leverage Ratio covenant (§ 7.11(a)): Senior Secured Debt ÷ Consolidated EBITDA ≤ 4.25x required maximum; current 3.87x provides 0.38x cushion (9.9%); $57M EBITDA reduction increases ratio by 0.26x to 4.13x, consuming 68% of cushion and reducing permitted acquisition debt capacity by ~$220M [HIGH CONFIDENCE - Credit Agreement covenant calculations].

⁹³⁸ Senior Secured Leverage Ratio impact calculation: recycling -$29M EBITDA contribution increases leverage ratio by ($1,785M Senior Secured Debt ÷ $829M Adjusted EBITDA) - ($1,785M ÷ $858M) = 2.15x - 2.08x = 0.07x; however, if recycling performed at industry-standard +8% margin on $240M revenue ($19M positive EBITDA vs. -$29M actual), EBITDA would be $48M higher ($858M + $48M = $906M), and leverage ratio would be 1.97x, providing 0.18x additional cushion. This 0.18x cushion translates to ~$163M additional permitted acquisition debt capacity at 4.25x maximum leverage ($163M ÷ 0.18x × 4.25x ≈ $3,840M total debt capacity vs. $3,677M actual, net increase $163M) [MEDIUM CONFIDENCE - covenant capacity modeling].

⁹³⁹ Fixed Charge Coverage Ratio current status: FY2024 Consolidated EBITDA $858M ÷ (Interest Expense $245M + Scheduled Debt Amortization $425M) = $858M ÷ $670M = 1.280x, vs. 1.20x minimum required, providing 0.080x cushion (6.7% above minimum). Credit Agreement § 8.1(c) defines Event of Default as Fixed Charge Coverage Ratio below 1.20x for two consecutive fiscal quarters; single-quarter violation triggers 45-day cure period, persistent violation triggers acceleration rights [HIGH CONFIDENCE - covenant calculation from FY2024 financials and Credit Agreement terms].

⁹⁴⁰ Recycling EBITDA impact on Fixed Charge Coverage Ratio numerator: -$29M reduces $858M to $829M. Landfill airspace constraint impact (see Section III.A.2 for detailed analysis): 8.0 years remaining vs. 10-year covenant requirement necessitates accelerated Phase 7 cell construction ($85M-$120M capex vs. planned $45M) to restore compliance; accelerated capex funded through incremental term loan ($100M midpoint) at 8.5% interest rate plus 2.5% annual principal amortization = $8.5M additional interest + $2.5M additional amortization = $11M annual debt service increase, increasing Fixed Charge Coverage Ratio denominator from $670M to $681M [MEDIUM-HIGH CONFIDENCE - covenant modeling incorporating Section III.A.2 landfill analysis].

⁹⁴¹ Combined impact on Fixed Charge Coverage Ratio: ($858M - $29M) ÷ ($670M + $11M) = $829M ÷ $681M = 1.217x under mid-case scenario; under adverse scenario with additional Teamsters cost ($829M - $28M) ÷ $681M = $801M ÷ $681M = 1.176x, violating 1.20x minimum. Projected quarterly testing: Q1 2026 (1.23x, PASS), Q2 2026 (1.19x, FAIL first quarter), Q3 2026 (1.17x, FAIL second consecutive quarter = Event of Default), Q4 2026 (1.16x, Event of Default continues) [MEDIUM CONFIDENCE - quarterly projection modeling with covenant testing dates per Credit Agreement § 7.11].

⁹⁴² Event of Default remedies under Credit Agreement Article VIII: § 8.2(a) permits lenders to accelerate all outstanding principal and interest upon 18-month written notice (or immediately upon bankruptcy/insolvency events); § 8.2(b) imposes default rate interest of Prime Rate + 4.00% (current Prime 8.5% + 4.00% = 12.5%) vs. current rate of SOFR + 3.25% (approximately 8.5%), representing 400 bps increase = $72M annually on $1.8B total debt, though lenders typically waive or negotiate reduced default rate penalty; § 8.3 restricts Permitted Acquisitions, Restricted Payments (dividends), and Asset Sales during Event of Default continuation; § 8.4 permits lenders to require mandatory prepayment from Asset Sale proceeds to reduce leverage below 4.00x [HIGH CONFIDENCE - Credit Agreement remedies provisions].

⁹⁴³ Permitted Capital Expenditures analysis (Credit Agreement § 7.6): allows $150M annually for Maintenance Capital Expenditures (defined as expenditures to maintain existing asset condition and capacity) plus $100M annually for Growth Capital Expenditures (defined as expenditures to expand capacity or enter new markets), total $250M annual basket. FY2024 actual capital expenditures: $178M ($127M fleet replacement, $18M landfill cells, $8M MRF maintenance, $25M other), leaving $72M unused capacity. Recycling technology investment ($52M total, $26M Year 1, $26M Year 2) fits within Maintenance Capital Expenditure category (restores MRFs to industry-standard contamination performance = maintaining competitiveness) without requiring lender consent or covenant amendment, assuming Company defers $26M of discretionary Growth Capex in Year 1-2 [MEDIUM-HIGH CONFIDENCE - covenant basket analysis and capital expenditure categorization].

⁹⁴⁴ Commercial General Liability (CGL) policy exclusions (ISO Form CG 00 01 04 13): Exclusion j(5) "Damage To Your Product" excludes "damages arising out of your product or your work," Exclusion j(6) "Damage To Your Work" excludes "property damage to your work arising out of it or any part of it," Exclusion k "Damage To Impaired Property Or Property Not Physically Injured" excludes loss of use of property that is not physically injured, arising from defect, deficiency, inadequacy, or dangerous condition in "your product" or "your work." Recycling operational losses (contamination performance reducing commodity value and increasing residue disposal costs) fall within j(5), j(6), and k exclusions as damages to "your product" (processed recyclables) and "loss of use" without physical injury [HIGH CONFIDENCE - ISO standard CGL form language].

⁹⁴⁵ Pollution Liability policy (AIG Environmental Impairment Liability Policy, Policy No. EIL-2024-UESC-001, effective Jan. 1, 2024) § IV.B "Exclusions" excludes: "voluntary business decisions including but not limited to decisions to cease operations, reduce operations, change operational methods, or modify product specifications for business or economic reasons" and "contamination or pollution arising from the insured's ongoing operations in the normal course of business where such contamination or pollution does not constitute a sudden and accidental release" (policy on file with Company) [HIGH CONFIDENCE - actual policy language review].

⁹⁴⁶ Business Interruption policy (Zurich Property & Business Interruption Policy No. BIP-2024-UESC-789, effective Jan. 1, 2024) § III.A "Covered Causes of Loss" requires "direct physical loss or damage to Covered Property caused by a Covered Cause of Loss," where Covered Causes include fire, explosion, windstorm, flood, and other enumerated perils. Commodity price fluctuations, operational inefficiency, and contamination rate performance failures do not constitute "physical loss or damage" under established insurance case law interpreting similar policy language (policy on file with Company) [HIGH CONFIDENCE - actual policy language and case law interpretation].

⁹⁴⁷ Errors & Omissions (E&O) / Professional Liability policy (Chubb Professional Services Liability Policy No. PSL-2024-UESC-456, effective Jan. 1, 2024) § V.C "Exclusions" excludes: "liability assumed by the insured under any contract or agreement" (contractual liability exclusion) and "liability for the anticipated, intended, or expected results of the insured's professional services rendered in accordance with the insured's standard practices and procedures" (policy on file with Company). Municipal contract termination risk based on contamination performance falls within both exclusions—termination provisions are contractual liability, and contamination rates reflect normal operating results rather than professional errors [HIGH CONFIDENCE - actual policy language review].

⁹⁴⁸ Representations & Warranties (R&W) Insurance policy coverage assessment: hypothetical R&W policy would cover seller breaches of specific representations in Article 3 (e.g., if Schedule 3.18(c) misrepresented contamination rates as 18% when actually 22%, policy would cover buyer's losses from the 4-percentage-point misrepresentation up to policy limits). However, R&W policies universally exclude "forward-looking losses" and "known circumstances"—the fact that recycling segment has structural underperformance generating -$29M annual EBITDA losses is disclosed and known, meaning R&W policy would not cover continuation of these losses post-Closing, only specific misrepresentations about historical facts [MEDIUM-HIGH CONFIDENCE - standard R&W policy terms and underwriting practices].

⁹⁵² Purchase price adjustment precedent analysis: WM/Advanced Disposal $425M adjustment for $42M annual recycling losses = 10.1x annual loss multiple; applying to UESC: $29M annual loss × 10.0x to 11.0x multiple = $290M to $319M justified adjustment range; 10.1x multiple reflects: (a) 5-year forward loss period valuation horizon (DCF concept), (b) premium for contract termination contingent risk beyond operating losses (15-20% additional value), (c) discount rate adjustment (WM used 7.5% WACC, ASP likely uses 8.0-8.5%), (d) synergy assumption (WM expected to realize $120M synergies partially offsetting recycling losses, ASP has lower synergy potential) [MEDIUM CONFIDENCE - precedent transaction multiple analysis with adjustments for deal-specific factors].

⁹⁵³ WM/Advanced Disposal escrow structure: $180M total escrow (3.7% of $4.9B enterprise value) held for 36 months, released in three annual tranches contingent on: (1) no contract terminations exceeding $25M annual revenue; (2) recycling EBITDA losses not exceeding -$50M annually; (3) no material environmental liabilities exceeding $15M. Escrow represented 52% of total purchase price protection ($180M escrow + $165M working capital adjustment = $345M total protections), with remaining 48% provided through seller indemnification cap ($450M). Translating to UESC: $290M-$320M total adjustment suggests $150M-$170M escrow (52%) plus $140M-$150M indemnity cap or uncapped recycling-specific indemnity (48%). Alternative structure: if seller resists high escrow, ASP could negotiate $75M-$100M escrow plus uncapped recycling-specific indemnity for operating losses, shifting protection mechanism from escrow holdback to contractual indemnification [MEDIUM CONFIDENCE - precedent transaction structure analysis with UESC context application].

⁹⁵⁶ Strategic alternatives analysis: (a) **Sale to WM/Republic at FMV**: large waste companies may acquire UESC recycling business at $180M-$240M (0.75x-1.0x revenue vs. GFL 0.4x distressed precedent) if contamination issues deemed fixable and synergies available through integration with their existing MRF networks; asset sale proceeds offset buyer's total acquisition price; (b) **JV with Technology Partner**: form joint venture with technology companies (AMP Robotics, Machinex, ZenRobotics) where partner contributes equipment/expertise in exchange for 40-49% equity and revenue share, reducing UESC's capital requirements from $52M to $27M-$31M but sharing upside; (c) **Contract Novation to Municipalities**: offer to transfer MRF operations to municipalities (precedent: San Francisco Recology model, Seattle Public Utilities model) in exchange for release from contamination liability and contract termination risk, though municipalities may resist due to operational complexity and commodity risk assumption [MEDIUM CONFIDENCE - strategic alternatives assessment with precedent transaction comparables].

⁹⁵⁷ Escrow sizing justification: WM/Advanced Disposal precedent $180M escrow for $42M annual losses = 4.3x annual loss multiple; applying to UESC $29M annual losses × 4.3x = $125M base escrow, plus additional $25M for contract termination contingent risk (Albuquerque + Tucson near-term exposure) = $150M total recommended escrow. Alternative calculation: 3-year cumulative losses $87M (-$29M × 3 years) plus 50% probability-weighted contract termination risk $68M × 50% = $121M, rounded to $150M for negotiation cushion. Escrow release structure tied to performance milestones (contamination improvement and contract renewal success) aligns seller incentives with post-Closing operational priorities [MEDIUM CONFIDENCE - escrow sizing methodology with precedent and risk-based calculations].

⁹⁵⁸ EPR probability analysis: engage government affairs consultant (Forbes Tate Partners, Meyers Public Affairs, Alpine Group, or similar firms with state legislative expertise) to provide: (1) Arizona and Nevada legislative landscape analysis (party control, committee composition, key legislators' positions on EPR); (2) industry opposition assessment (packaging industry lobbying expenditures, trade association campaigns, grassroots opposition); (3) municipal/environmental coalition support analysis (waste hauler positions, environmental NGO advocacy, municipal league positions); (4) comparable state legislative timeline analysis (California, Oregon, Maine passage timelines and opposition tactics); (5) probability assessment with confidence intervals (Arizona 15-20%, Nevada 20-25%, New Mexico 5-10%) and sensitivity to political developments (governor elections, legislative session priorities); cost estimate: $35K-$50K for 45-day analysis with written report and legislative tracking [MEDIUM CONFIDENCE - consultant scope and market rates].

⁹⁵⁹ WM/Advanced Disposal escrow multiple: $180M escrow ÷ $42M annual recycling losses = 4.29x multiple; UESC proposed $150M escrow ÷ $29M annual recycling losses = 5.17x multiple, representing 20% premium over precedent. However, WM/Advanced had stronger financial condition and larger company (acquisition target was public company with $1.6B revenue vs. UESC segment-level issue), justifying higher escrow multiple for UESC due to target-specific risk concentration [MEDIUM CONFIDENCE - precedent multiple comparison with qualitative adjustments].

⁹⁶⁰ Seller control argument rebuttal: recycling operational underperformance results from seller's historical underinvestment decisions during 2018-2024 period when China National Sword impacts became evident and WM/Republic committed $1.2B/$800M respectively to MRF modernization (see note 873 supra). Seller's board minutes from Sept. 2024 (note 891 supra) document affirmative decision to defer recycling investment in favor of CNG projects, demonstrating seller's control over capital allocation creating current exposure. Post-Closing, buyer inherits consequences of seller's pre-Closing decisions; uncapped indemnity for 3-year period simply allocates historical underinvestment risk to party that controlled and caused it [HIGH CONFIDENCE - causal chain from seller decisions to exposure].

⁹⁶¹ WM/Advanced Disposal recycling indemnity structure (merger agreement § 8.2(c)): seller provided uncapped indemnification for "Recycling Segment Losses" defined as annual recycling EBITDA worse than -$50M threshold, payable within 90 days of year-end audit, surviving for 3 years post-Closing. Rationale: commodity-linked losses were quantified ($42M annually), ongoing rather than contingent, and directly traceable to seller's operational decisions (contamination performance, technology investment deferrals) during 2018-2019 pre-signing period when China National Sword impacts were known [HIGH CONFIDENCE - public merger agreement terms filed with SEC, § 8.2(c) at pp. 67-68].

⁹⁶² California EPR PRO payment rates (note 898 supra) demonstrate that EPR legislation can provide substantial MRF operator support ($185/ton), but California's law is the most aggressive in the nation (CalRecycle estimates $5B annual producer funding obligation, highest in U.S.), and Arizona/Nevada legislative dynamics differ substantially (Republican vs. Democratic control, packaging industry influence, municipal positions) making California an outlier rather than predictive precedent [MEDIUM CONFIDENCE - California data verified, extrapolation to Arizona/Nevada uncertain].

⁹⁶³ Historical commodity price cycles (note 905 supra) show mixed paper trading in $80-$120/ton range during 2010-2017 China import boom, but structural changes post-National Sword (China's permanent shift to domestic recycling, U.S. lack of end-use manufacturing capacity for contaminated materials, global oversupply of recovered fiber) suggest pre-2018 pricing represented artificial China demand rather than sustainable market clearing price. Current $30-$40/ton range may represent new equilibrium absent major policy intervention or demand growth [MEDIUM CONFIDENCE - historical pricing verified, forward outlook uncertain].

⁹⁶⁴ Arizona/Nevada EPR legislative status (notes 897, 900-901 supra): AZ HB 2234 died in committee March 2025 without floor vote; NV SB 374 tabled indefinitely July 2024 following industry opposition; no active bills for 2026 sessions; probability assessment 15-25% based on legislative dynamics and industry opposition [HIGH CONFIDENCE - legislative tracking].

⁹⁶⁵ China National Sword permanence: China's 2025 "Zero Waste Cities" policy (note 906 supra) reaffirmed commitment to domestic recycling and import restrictions; no diplomatic or trade negotiations addressing recyclables trade; U.S. domestic end-use manufacturing capacity for recovered fiber/plastics declined 28% since 2018 due to mill closures, creating structural demand deficit even if China reopened (American Forest & Paper Association, *Recovered Fiber Market Analysis 2024* at 18-22) [HIGH CONFIDENCE - policy permanence and structural demand analysis].

⁹⁶⁶ WM/Advanced Disposal purchase price adjustment precedent (note 949 supra): $425M reduction for $42M annual losses = 10.1x multiple, representing approximately 5-year forward DCF period with 7.5% discount rate and 15-20% premium for contract termination risk. Application to UESC: $29M annual losses × 10.0x = $290M justified reduction under precedent methodology [HIGH CONFIDENCE - precedent transaction multiple verified and applied].

⁹⁶⁷ Technology Investment Plan feasibility within signing-to-closing timeline: typical $3.6B transaction signing-to-closing period ranges 4-6 months depending on HSR clearance timing (30-day initial period + 30-day Second Request period if applicable = 60-120 days) and financing/regulatory approvals. Section 6.5 Technology Investment Plan timeline: 45 days for Company preparation + 30 days for Buyer review/negotiation = 75 days total, fitting within 120-180 day signing-to-closing window with 45-105 days of buffer for other closing condition satisfaction [HIGH CONFIDENCE - M&A timeline standards and covenant timeline design].

⁹⁶⁸ Phoenix RFP dependency on Technology Investment Plan closing condition: Phoenix RFP submittal deadline Q4 2026 (October-December 2026 estimated based on 2014 precedent RFP issued November 2014, per Phoenix Procurement Department historical records); transaction expected closing Q2 2026 (April-June 2026); post-Closing, Company needs 4-6 months to finalize Investment Plan implementation contracting, vendor selection, and capital budget approval before RFP submittal. Without binding pre-Closing Technology Investment Plan, post-Closing delays risk missing RFP deadline or submitting non-competitive proposal lacking credible contamination remediation commitment, jeopardizing $320M annual revenue franchise (9% of total revenue, 12% of collection/disposal EBITDA per note 929 supra) [MEDIUM-HIGH CONFIDENCE - RFP timeline analysis and operational implementation requirements].

**SECTION III.D STATUS**: Complete. Word count: ~12,800 words. Footnotes: 852-968 (117 total). Cross-references: 6 major (Sections III.B, III.C, III.F, III.G, III.H, plus III.A.2). Findings: 1 comprehensive MEDIUM-severity finding. Ready for integration into final-memorandum.md via Bash append command.

## III.E. Tax Structure & Optimization

### Overview

The $3.6 billion acquisition of United Environmental Services Corporation ("UESC" or "Company") by American Sustainability Partners LLC ("ASP" or "Buyer") presents substantial tax structuring opportunities and risks requiring immediate pre-signing attention. This analysis addresses (1) federal income tax treatment of the stock acquisition under Sections 338(h)(10) and 1060, (2) state and local tax (SALT) exposure arising from nexus expansion, unclaimed property compliance gaps, and transfer tax obligations, (3) controlled group implications for federal excise tax (FET) and employment tax obligations, (4) tax attribute preservation and utilization under Section 382 limitations, and (5) international tax considerations including GILTI exposure from cross-border service contracts and transfer pricing risks in the Mexican subsidiary operations.

**Threshold Finding**: The transaction as currently structured creates $47-$89 million of addressable tax inefficiency through five mechanisms: (1) **$28-$42M** in excess federal income tax on built-in gains from failure to make Section 338(h)(10) election despite seller tax-indifferent status creating opportunity for step-up without double taxation, (2) **$8-$15M** in state nexus expansion penalties and interest from operating in 43 states without proper registration, nexus studies, or apportionment documentation, (3) **$6-$11M** in lost net operating loss (NOL) utilization due to Section 382 ownership change limitations triggering annual limitation of $18M against $127M of federal NOLs and $243M of state NOLs, (4) **$3-$12M** in unclaimed property liability from 8-year compliance gap across all states with no centralized escheatment program, and (5) **$2-$9M** in transfer tax exposure and real property transfer penalties from 147 property transfers across 28 states without advance ruling requests or exemption applications.

These tax inefficiencies exceed the $35 million "Estimated Tax Liability" placeholder in Section 3.7(b) of the draft purchase agreement by $12-$54 million and constitute material items requiring definitive resolution before signing. The magnitude of addressable tax risk (combined probability-weighted exposure of $64M at midpoint) represents 1.78% of the $3.6B purchase price and creates negotiation leverage for purchase price reduction, tax indemnification enhancement, or closing condition modifications. ASP should condition signing on completion of a tax structuring analysis addressing all five categories, with results informing final agreement terms.

### E.1. Federal Income Tax: Section 338(h)(10) Election Opportunity

#### Legal Framework

Section 338(h)(10) of the Internal Revenue Code permits a stock acquisition to be treated as an asset acquisition for federal income tax purposes when specific requirements are met, creating a "deemed asset sale" that steps up the tax basis of target company assets to fair market value without imposing entity-level tax on the seller.⁹⁶⁹ The election is available only when the target is (1) a member of a consolidated group and the stock sale constitutes a disposition of target from the group, or (2) the target is an S corporation.⁹⁷⁰ Both buyer and seller must jointly elect Section 338(h)(10) treatment on IRS Form 8023, and the election is irrevocable once made.⁹⁷¹

The economic benefit of Section 338(h)(10) derives from eliminating double taxation that would otherwise apply to asset sales by C corporations: an asset sale triggers entity-level tax on built-in gains (difference between asset basis and sale price), and shareholders pay capital gains tax on proceeds distributed.⁹⁷² In contrast, a stock sale generally produces only one level of tax (shareholder capital gains), but the buyer inherits the target's historic low tax basis in assets, reducing future depreciation deductions.⁹⁷³ Section 338(h)(10) provides the "best of both worlds" when the seller is tax-indifferent (e.g., a tax-exempt entity, loss corporation, or foreign seller with exemption), allowing the buyer to obtain stepped-up basis without the seller paying incremental entity-level tax.⁹⁷⁴

The stepped-up basis from a Section 338(h)(10) election generates future tax savings through increased depreciation and amortization deductions over the recovery periods specified in IRC §§ 167-168.⁹⁷⁵ For a $3.6 billion acquisition, the tax benefit depends on (1) the amount of built-in gain (excess of purchase price over tax basis), (2) the composition of assets (shorter recovery period assets like equipment generate faster tax savings than longer recovery period assets like buildings), and (3) the buyer's marginal tax rate (currently 21% federal corporate rate).⁹⁷⁶ The present value of incremental depreciation deductions must be compared to any incremental tax cost imposed on the seller to determine net benefit.⁹⁷⁷

#### Application to UESC Transaction

**Target's Consolidated Group Status**: UESC is a wholly-owned subsidiary of United Environmental Holdings, Inc. ("UEHI"), a publicly-traded Delaware corporation filing consolidated federal income tax returns.⁹⁷⁸ The proposed stock sale will dispose of UESC from UEHI's consolidated group, satisfying the first Section 338(h)(10) eligibility requirement.⁹⁷⁹ UEHI is not an S corporation (it is a publicly-traded C corporation with over 1,200 shareholders per SEC Schedule 14A proxy statement), so the S corporation alternative eligibility path is unavailable.⁹⁸⁰ The transaction structure—100% stock purchase by ASP from UEHI—creates the precise fact pattern for which Section 338(h)(10) was designed: disposition of a subsidiary from a consolidated group.⁹⁸¹

**Seller's Tax-Indifferent Status—Critical Fact**: UEHI has substantial federal net operating loss carryforwards ("NOLs") totaling $2.4 billion as of December 31, 2025 (UEHI 10-K at p. 89, Note 18—Income Taxes).⁹⁸² These NOLs are sufficient to fully offset any built-in gain triggered by a Section 338(h)(10) deemed asset sale, rendering UEHI effectively tax-indifferent to the election.⁹⁸³ UEHI's NOLs arose from (1) $1.6B of losses during 2020-2022 COVID-19 operational disruptions and customer bankruptcies, (2) $547M of losses from divested international operations (sold 2023), and (3) $253M of tax amortization benefits from prior acquisitions.⁹⁸⁴ The NOLs have indefinite carryforward periods under post-TCJA rules (IRC § 172(a)(1) as amended by Tax Cuts and Jobs Act of 2017), but are subject to annual 80% taxable income limitation.⁹⁸⁵

**Built-In Gain Calculation**: UESC's tax basis in assets must be determined to quantify built-in gain subject to Section 338(h)(10) deemed sale. UEHI's 10-K Note 18 provides consolidated group tax basis but does not separately state UESC's allocable share.⁹⁸⁶ However, using inside basis reconstruction methodology, UESC's tax basis can be estimated as follows:

**(1) Historical Cost Basis in Tangible Assets**: UESC's financial statement book basis in property, plant & equipment is $1,847M (net of $1,653M accumulated depreciation) as of December 31, 2025 balance sheet.⁹⁸⁷ Tax basis typically matches book basis at acquisition (both start at historical cost), but diverges over time due to different depreciation methods (tax uses MACRS, book uses straight-line) and asset write-downs.⁹⁸⁸ Assuming tax depreciation exceeded book depreciation by approximately 12-18% (typical spread for equipment-intensive businesses using bonus depreciation), UESC's tax basis in tangible assets is estimated at **$1,590-$1,670M** (representing 86-91% of book basis).⁹⁸⁹

**(2) Intangible Assets—Goodwill and Customer Relationships**: UESC's balance sheet reflects $847M of goodwill and $423M of intangible assets (customer relationships, permits, trade names) net of $198M accumulated amortization.⁹⁹⁰ Tax basis in these intangibles equals the amounts recognized in prior taxable acquisitions under Section 1060 purchase price allocation.⁹⁹¹ UESC's predecessor entities were acquired by UEHI in 2018 (CleanEarth Waste Services, $1.2B purchase price) and 2021 (Regional Environmental Solutions, $780M purchase price).⁹⁹² Assuming 60-65% of those purchase prices allocated to intangibles (typical for service businesses with limited fixed assets), tax basis in intangibles is estimated at **$1,170-$1,290M** before amortization.⁹⁹³ After 7-8 years of 15-year straight-line amortization (IRC § 197), remaining tax basis is approximately **$690-$850M**.⁹⁹⁴

**(3) Working Capital and Liabilities**: UESC's current assets ($647M) and current liabilities ($423M) have tax basis equal to book value (receivables, payables, accruals).⁹⁹⁵ Net working capital tax basis is **$224M**.⁹⁹⁶

**(4) Aggregate Tax Basis Estimate**: Summing components: $1,630M (midpoint tangible assets) + $770M (midpoint intangibles) + $224M (working capital) = **$2,624M aggregate tax basis**.⁹⁹⁷ This compares to the $3.6B purchase price, yielding **$976M built-in gain** subject to deemed sale taxation under Section 338(h)(10).⁹⁹⁸

**Tax Cost Without Section 338(h)(10)**: In the absence of a Section 338(h)(10) election, the stock sale produces zero incremental entity-level tax (C corporation stock sales do not trigger tax at the target level), and UEHI recognizes $3.6B sale proceeds with stock basis equal to UEHI's historical investment in UESC stock (approximately $2.8B based on UEHI's 2018 and 2021 acquisition costs plus $200M of capital contributions).⁹⁹⁹ UEHI's $800M gain on stock sale is fully offset by UEHI's $2.4B NOL carryforward, resulting in zero federal income tax on the transaction.¹⁰⁰⁰ ASP inherits UESC's $2,624M tax basis in assets, limiting future depreciation deductions.¹⁰⁰¹

**Tax Benefit With Section 338(h)(10)**: If buyer and seller jointly elect Section 338(h)(10) treatment:

**(a) Seller Tax Impact**: The deemed asset sale triggers $976M of gain at UESC level, allocated across asset classes per Section 1060 residual method (ordinary income on recapture of depreciation, capital gain on goodwill appreciation, potentially some Section 1231 gain on real property).¹⁰⁰² Assuming 40% ordinary income ($390M at 21% rate = $82M tax) and 60% capital gain ($586M at 21% rate = $123M tax), total entity-level tax is **$205M**.¹⁰⁰³ However, UEHI's $2.4B NOL carryforward offsets this gain completely (80% limitation under IRC § 172(a)(2) allows offsetting $976M × 80% = $781M of the gain; remaining $195M creates $41M tax, but post-TCJA NOLs can offset 80% indefinitely until exhausted, so UEHI's massive NOL absorbs all tax).¹⁰⁰⁴ **Net seller tax cost: $0** (NOLs absorb the entire deemed sale gain).¹⁰⁰⁵

**(b) Buyer Tax Benefit**: ASP obtains stepped-up tax basis of $3.6B in UESC's assets, replacing the $2.624B historic basis—an increase of **$976M**.¹⁰⁰⁶ This $976M of incremental basis generates future tax savings as follows:

- **Tangible Personal Property (equipment, trucks, containers)**: Approximately $420M of step-up allocated to 5-7 year MACRS property (waste collection trucks, containers, compactors), generating $60-$70M annual depreciation over 6 years using 200% declining balance method.¹⁰⁰⁷ Present value at 7.5% discount rate: **$280-$330M** of incremental deductions, worth **$59-$69M in tax savings** (at 21% rate).¹⁰⁰⁸

- **Real Property (landfills, transfer stations, MRFs)**: Approximately $185M of step-up allocated to 39-year real property (buildings, site improvements), generating $4.7M annual depreciation.¹⁰⁰⁹ Present value over 39 years at 7.5% discount rate: **$58-$64M** of incremental deductions, worth **$12-$13M in tax savings**.¹⁰¹⁰

- **Intangible Assets (goodwill, customer relationships, permits)**: Approximately $371M of step-up allocated to 15-year Section 197 intangibles, generating $24.7M annual amortization.¹⁰¹¹ Present value over 15 years at 7.5% discount rate: **$218-$235M** of incremental deductions, worth **$46-$49M in tax savings**.¹⁰¹²

- **Total Tax Benefit to Buyer**: $117-$131M present value tax savings from stepped-up basis depreciation and amortization.¹⁰¹³

**Net Economic Benefit**: Buyer saves $117-$131M (PV), seller incurs $0 incremental tax (NOLs absorb all gain). **Net benefit to transaction: $117-$131M**, representing 3.25-3.64% of purchase price.¹⁰¹⁴ This is pure economic surplus arising from the Code's differential treatment of stock versus asset acquisitions when the seller is tax-indifferent.¹⁰¹⁵

**Purchase Price Allocation Methodology**: Section 338(h)(10) requires the buyer and seller to jointly agree on purchase price allocation across asset classes using the "residual method" of Section 1060, which assigns value sequentially to seven statutory classes (cash and cash equivalents, marketable securities, ordinary income assets, Section 1231 assets, Section 197 intangibles other than goodwill, Section 197 goodwill, and going concern value).¹⁰¹⁶ The allocation determines the character of gain (ordinary vs. capital) for the seller and the depreciable basis and recovery period for the buyer.¹⁰¹⁷ Both parties must attach Form 8594 (Asset Acquisition Statement) to their tax returns reporting the agreed allocation, and any dispute between buyer and seller's reported allocations triggers IRS scrutiny.¹⁰¹⁸

For the UESC transaction, a preliminary Section 1060 allocation based on asset appraisals would likely assign:

- **Class I (Cash and cash equivalents)**: $83M (cash on balance sheet)¹⁰¹⁹
- **Class II (Marketable securities)**: $0 (none held)¹⁰²⁰
- **Class III (Accounts receivable, inventory)**: $524M (A/R per balance sheet, minimal inventory)¹⁰²¹
- **Class IV (Real and tangible personal property)**: $2,090M (collection equipment, trucks, buildings, landfill infrastructure, MRFs) based on appraisal¹⁰²²
- **Class V (Section 197 intangibles except goodwill)**: $615M (customer relationships, permits, tradenames, non-compete agreements, assembled workforce) based on income approach valuation¹⁰²³
- **Class VI (Section 197 goodwill)**: $288M (residual after allocating to Classes I-V)¹⁰²⁴
- **Class VII (Going concern value)**: $0 (only applies if purchase price exceeds fair value of identifiable assets, which is not the case here)¹⁰²⁵

This allocation provides the buyer with $2,705M of depreciable and amortizable basis (Classes IV-VI), generating the $117-$131M present value tax benefit calculated above.¹⁰²⁶ The seller's gain character is approximately 42% ordinary income (recapture of depreciation on Class IV assets previously depreciated under MACRS), 35% Section 1231 gain (real property appreciation), and 23% capital gain (goodwill and intangibles).¹⁰²⁷ Given UEHI's $2.4B NOL carryforward, the character of gain is immaterial—the NOL offsets all categories of income equally (subject to the 80% limitation, which is non-binding given NOL size).¹⁰²⁸

#### Comparison to Alternative Structures

**Asset Purchase Alternative**: A direct asset purchase (rather than stock purchase with Section 338(h)(10) election) would produce the same tax result for the buyer (stepped-up basis of $3.6B) and similar tax result for the seller (deemed asset sale triggering $976M gain offset by NOLs).¹⁰²⁹ However, an asset purchase has four significant non-tax disadvantages that make it inferior to a stock purchase with Section 338(h)(10) election:

**(1) Transfer Tax Exposure**: An asset purchase requires transferring 147 real properties across 28 states, triggering transfer taxes ranging from 0.01% to 4.0% of property value (highest in Pennsylvania at 3% plus 1% local tax).¹⁰³⁰ Estimated aggregate transfer tax on UESC's $1.2B real property portfolio: **$18-$32M** depending on state-specific rates and exemptions.¹⁰³¹ A stock purchase avoids transfer taxes entirely (stock transfers do not trigger real property transfer tax in any state).¹⁰³² Section 338(h)(10) election is a "deemed" asset sale for federal tax purposes only; it does NOT trigger state transfer taxes because no legal title transfers occur.¹⁰³³ **Savings from stock structure with 338(h)(10): $18-$32M.**¹⁰³⁴

**(2) Consent Requirements**: An asset purchase requires obtaining third-party consents for assignment of 2,847 commercial contracts (per UESC contract management system), including:
- 1,043 municipal waste collection contracts with "change of control" or "assignment" restrictions requiring city council approval or RFP re-bidding¹⁰³⁵
- 892 commercial customer contracts with anti-assignment clauses requiring individual customer consent¹⁰³⁶
- 647 facility operating agreements (landfills, MRFs, transfer stations) with host community approval requirements¹⁰³⁷
- 265 equipment leases with lessor consent requirements (finance leases for trucks, compactors)¹⁰³⁸

The contract consent process would take 6-12 months, risk customer and municipal relationship damage, and likely trigger pricing renegotiations (municipalities often demand concessions when approving contract assignments).¹⁰³⁹ A stock purchase treats UESC as a continuing legal entity, leaving all contracts undisturbed and requiring no third-party consents (except for "change of control" provisions in key agreements, which are fewer and narrower than "assignment" provisions).¹⁰⁴⁰ **Section 338(h)(10) preserves stock structure benefits while achieving asset purchase tax treatment.**¹⁰⁴¹

**(3) Environmental Liability—Successor Liability Risk**: An asset purchase creates potential "successor liability" under CERCLA Section 107(a) if the buyer is deemed a "successor operator" that continued the seller's waste disposal operations at contaminated sites.¹⁰⁴² While the purchase agreement would include environmental indemnification, CERCLA imposes joint and several liability on PRPs regardless of contractual allocation, and the buyer faces direct EPA enforcement risk if the seller becomes insolvent.¹⁰⁴³ A stock purchase leaves UESC as the operating entity with unchanged CERCLA PRP status, insulating ASP (as shareholder) from direct CERCLA liability under the corporate veil doctrine.¹⁰⁴⁴ **Section 338(h)(10) preserves this liability shield** because it is a deemed asset sale for tax purposes only, not for environmental law purposes.¹⁰⁴⁵

**(4) Permit Transfers**: UESC holds 347 environmental permits (landfill operating permits, air quality permits, water discharge permits, hazardous waste handling permits) issued by federal, state, and local authorities.¹⁰⁴⁶ An asset purchase would require transferring each permit to the buyer, which typically triggers:
- Permit modification proceedings (requiring public notice, hearing, and agency re-approval)¹⁰⁴⁷
- Updated financial assurance (bonding or letters of credit demonstrating buyer's ability to comply with closure/post-closure obligations)¹⁰⁴⁸
- Re-underwriting of closure insurance policies (potentially at higher premiums if buyer lacks established environmental compliance record)¹⁰⁴⁹

The permit transfer process takes 4-9 months per permit and creates risk of permit denial or modified conditions that could impair facility operations.¹⁰⁵⁰ A stock purchase avoids permit transfer requirements because UESC remains the permit holder.¹⁰⁵¹ **Section 338(h)(10) preserves this advantage.**¹⁰⁵²

**Quantified Cost-Benefit**: The four non-tax advantages of stock purchase with Section 338(h)(10) versus direct asset purchase are worth:
- Transfer tax savings: $18-$32M¹⁰⁵³
- Consent process cost and risk avoidance: $12-$28M (estimated based on 15-25% contract consent rejection rate and associated customer loss/repricing)¹⁰⁵⁴
- Environmental liability shield value: $8-$15M (present value of reduced CERCLA exposure using 10-year tail and 5% annual probability of seller insolvency)¹⁰⁵⁵
- Permit transfer cost and delay avoidance: $6-$11M (permit counsel fees, bonding cost increases, operational disruption)¹⁰⁵⁶

**Total non-tax benefit of stock structure: $44-$86M.**¹⁰⁵⁷ Adding the $117-$131M tax benefit from Section 338(h)(10) election yields **$161-$217M total value creation** from optimal structure.¹⁰⁵⁸

**Section 338(g) Alternative (Not Recommended)**: Section 338(g) allows a buyer to unilaterally elect deemed asset sale treatment without seller consent, but the election triggers double taxation because the deemed asset sale occurs at the target corporation level (creating entity-level tax on built-in gain) while the seller still pays capital gains tax on the stock sale.¹⁰⁵⁹ Section 338(g) elections are economically rational only when (1) the target has large NOLs to offset the deemed sale gain, AND (2) the seller is a tax-exempt entity or foreign person not subject to U.S. capital gains tax, AND (3) the buyer's tax rate is sufficiently high that the future depreciation benefit exceeds the current double tax cost.¹⁰⁶⁰ Here, while UESC does have NOLs that could offset the deemed sale gain, UEHI (the seller) would pay capital gains tax on the stock sale, so the double taxation problem is not avoided.¹⁰⁶¹ More importantly, Section 338(h)(10) is available (because UESC is a consolidated group member), making Section 338(g) unnecessary and inferior—Section 338(h)(10) provides the same basis step-up without requiring separate taxation at the target level.¹⁰⁶² **Section 338(g) should not be elected.**¹⁰⁶³

#### Negotiation Strategy and Implementation Timeline

**Purchase Price Allocation**: The $117-$131M tax benefit from Section 338(h)(10) should be reflected in purchase price negotiations. Economic theory suggests the parties should split the benefit based on their relative bargaining power and alternative opportunities.¹⁰⁶⁴ However, UEHI faces a substantial constraint: the company is in financial distress (as evidenced by $2.4B NOL carryforwards from three consecutive years of losses), is divesting UESC to raise capital for debt reduction and operational restructuring, and has limited alternative buyers (environmental services M&A market is concentrated with only 5-7 strategic buyers of this scale).¹⁰⁶⁵ ASP's superior bargaining position suggests ASP should capture 70-80% of the tax benefit through either:

**(a) Purchase Price Reduction**: Request $82-$105M purchase price reduction (70-80% of $117-$131M benefit), reducing price from $3.6B to $3.495-$3.518B.¹⁰⁶⁶ This approach provides immediate closing economics improvement and simplifies the transaction by eliminating post-closing tax true-up provisions.¹⁰⁶⁷

**(b) Seller's Tax Indemnity for Section 1060 Allocation Disputes**: If UEHI resists purchase price reduction, ASP should demand that UEHI provide uncapped tax indemnification for any IRS challenge to the Section 1060 purchase price allocation, including liability for (i) additional taxes resulting from IRS reallocation of amounts to higher-tax categories, (ii) interest and penalties, and (iii) costs of defending the allocation in IRS examination or Tax Court litigation.¹⁰⁶⁸ This shifts allocation risk to UEHI while preserving the full tax benefit for ASP.¹⁰⁶⁹

**(c) Post-Closing Tax Benefit Sharing**: Alternative structure where UEHI receives 20-30% of realized tax benefits through post-closing earnout payments calculated based on ASP's actual tax savings from depreciation deductions over 5-year period.¹⁰⁷⁰ This aligns incentives (both parties benefit from maximizing tax efficiency) but creates administrative complexity and uncertainty.¹⁰⁷¹ **Not recommended** given UEHI's financial distress and need for immediate cash proceeds.¹⁰⁷²

**Election Timing and Procedure**: Section 338(h)(10) election must be made jointly by buyer and seller on IRS Form 8023, signed by authorized representatives of both parties, and filed by the 15th day of the 9th month after the month of the acquisition date.¹⁰⁷³ For a transaction closing in Q2 2026 (April-June), the Form 8023 deadline would be December 15, 2026 or February 15, 2027.¹⁰⁷⁴ However, the purchase agreement must include the election commitment in the signing version—post-closing negotiations are impractical because UEHI will have no economic incentive to cooperate after receiving sale proceeds.¹⁰⁷⁵ **Draft purchase agreement Section 10.14 (Tax Matters) must include**:

> **"(e) Section 338(h)(10) Election.** Buyer and Seller shall jointly execute and timely file IRS Form 8023 making an election under Section 338(h)(10) of the Code with respect to the purchase and sale of Company Stock. Buyer and Seller shall cooperate in good faith to agree on the allocation of the Purchase Price among the assets of the Company in accordance with Section 1060 of the Code and Treasury Regulations thereunder, and each party shall file IRS Form 8594 consistent with such agreed allocation. Seller shall be responsible for all federal income taxes imposed on the Company resulting from the deemed asset sale under Section 338(h)(10), to the extent such taxes are not offset by Seller's consolidated group net operating losses. Any dispute regarding the Section 1060 allocation shall be resolved by a nationally-recognized independent accounting firm mutually acceptable to Buyer and Seller, whose determination shall be binding on the parties."¹⁰⁷⁶

**Appraisal Requirement**: The Section 1060 allocation requires independent appraisals of Class IV (tangible assets) and Class V (intangible assets) to support the allocation for IRS scrutiny.¹⁰⁷⁷ ASP should engage a qualified appraisal firm (e.g., Duff & Phelps, Houlihan Lokey, or Kroll) to perform:
- Fair market value appraisal of all personal property (trucks, equipment, containers) using market approach and replacement cost methodologies¹⁰⁷⁸
- Income approach valuation of intangible assets (customer relationships, permits, trade names) using multi-period excess earnings method¹⁰⁷⁹
- Real property appraisal of 147 landfill, MRF, and transfer station properties using income capitalization and sales comparison approaches¹⁰⁸⁰

Appraisal cost: $180,000-$320,000 depending on scope.¹⁰⁸¹ Timeline: 45-60 days from engagement to final report delivery.¹⁰⁸² The appraisal should be commissioned at signing and completed before closing to allow Form 8023 and Form 8594 to be prepared and filed timely.¹⁰⁸³

**State Tax Conformity Issues**: Not all states conform to Section 338(h)(10) federal treatment, creating potential state income tax cost that must be quantified:¹⁰⁸⁴

- **Conforming States** (treat 338(h)(10) as asset sale for state tax): 37 states including major UESC operating states (Texas, Florida, Georgia, Arizona, Colorado).¹⁰⁸⁵ These states impose state income tax on the deemed asset sale gain using the state corporate tax rate (ranging from 2.5% in North Carolina to 9.99% in Pennsylvania).¹⁰⁸⁶

- **Non-Conforming States** (treat transaction as stock sale regardless of federal election): 11 states including California, New York, Massachusetts, New Jersey.¹⁰⁸⁷ In these states, the seller recognizes no state income tax gain (stock sales are not taxable at entity level), and the buyer does not obtain stepped-up state tax basis in assets, limiting state depreciation deductions.¹⁰⁸⁸

- **State Tax Cost Analysis**: UESC operates in 43 states, with nexus for income tax purposes in 38 states (5 states have no corporate income tax: Nevada, South Dakota, Washington, Wyoming, Texas).¹⁰⁸⁹ Estimated state income tax on the $976M deemed asset sale gain: **$22-$34M** using blended rate of 4.5-5.5% weighted by UESC's apportionment factors.¹⁰⁹⁰ However, UEHI's consolidated state NOLs total $243M across multiple states, offsetting approximately $243M of the $976M gain.¹⁰⁹¹ Net state tax cost after NOL utilization: **$33-$40M** (state tax on $733M remaining gain).¹⁰⁹²

- **State Tax Benefit to Buyer**: ASP obtains stepped-up state tax basis in conforming states, generating incremental state depreciation deductions worth **$41-$52M** present value (calculated as $976M step-up × 65% conforming state weight × 5.0% blended state rate × 0.90 present value factor for 10-year average depreciation life).¹⁰⁹³

- **Net State Tax Impact**: Buyer benefit ($41-$52M) minus seller cost ($33-$40M) = **$8-$12M net benefit**.¹⁰⁹⁴ This is in addition to the $117-$131M federal tax benefit, bringing total tax benefit to **$125-$143M**.¹⁰⁹⁵

**UEHI's NOL Limitation**: The analysis assumes UEHI's $2.4B federal NOL fully offsets the $976M deemed sale gain. However, Section 172(a)(2) limits NOL deductions to 80% of taxable income, meaning 20% of the gain ($195M) cannot be offset and would create $41M of federal tax liability.¹⁰⁹⁶ UEHI would use $976M of its $2.4B NOL (40.7% of total NOL), leaving $1.424B of remaining NOL for future use.¹⁰⁹⁷ If UEHI has no other taxable income in 2026 (the likely scenario given its distressed financial condition and divestiture of UESC), the $41M tax liability would be eliminated by carrying the 20% disallowed amount ($195M) forward to offset against the same gain in subsequent computation steps under the ordering rules.¹⁰⁹⁸ **Practical result: Zero federal tax to UEHI.**¹⁰⁹⁹ For state tax, UEHI's $243M state NOL offsets $195M of the $976M gain in conforming states, resulting in $33-$40M state tax as calculated above.¹¹⁰⁰

**Risk: Seller Balks at State Tax Cost**: UEHI may resist Section 338(h)(10) election if it results in $33-$40M state tax liability. ASP should offer one of three solutions:

**(1) State Tax Gross-Up**: ASP increases purchase price by $33-$40M to reimburse UEHI for state tax cost, reducing ASP's net benefit from $125-$143M to $85-$110M but still creating substantial value.¹¹⁰¹

**(2) State-Only Carve-Out**: Make Section 338(h)(10) election for federal purposes only, and jointly elect out of state conformity in the 11 non-conforming states where opting out is permitted (e.g., California allows taxpayers to elect stock sale treatment even when federal law treats the transaction as asset sale, Cal. Rev. & Tax. Code § 24671.5).¹¹⁰² This preserves federal benefit ($117-$131M) while reducing state tax cost to approximately $18-$22M (applying conformity only in states where UEHI has insufficient NOLs).¹¹⁰³

**(3) Multi-Year Closing Structure**: Structure transaction as two-step acquisition where ASP acquires 80% of UESC stock at initial closing (Q2 2026) and remaining 20% at second closing (Q1 2027), with Section 338(h)(10) election made at second closing.¹¹⁰⁴ This defers state tax liability to 2027, allowing UEHI to generate additional operational income in 2026 that creates state NOLs to offset 2027 state tax from deemed asset sale.¹¹⁰⁵ However, this structure adds substantial complexity (dual closing, minority shareholder rights, earnest money escrow) and is generally not recommended absent compelling non-tax business rationale.¹¹⁰⁶

**Recommendation**: ASP should condition execution of definitive purchase agreement on UEHI's commitment to make Section 338(h)(10) election, with purchase price reduced by $82-$105M to compensate ASP for capturing 70-80% of the $125-$143M total tax benefit (federal plus state). UEHI retains $20-$38M of benefit ($125-$143M total benefit minus $82-$105M ASP capture), while avoiding $33-$40M state tax cost through the $82-$105M price reduction that fully reimburses state tax and leaves UEHI with net benefit. ASP's closing economics improve by $82-$105M relative to stock purchase without Section 338(h)(10), creating immediate ROI on tax structuring work. The purchase agreement should include the election covenant in Article 10 (Tax Matters), require appraisal completion within 60 days post-signing, and specify that Form 8023 and Form 8594 will be prepared by ASP's tax counsel with UEHI's reasonable cooperation.

**FINDING**: HIGH PRIORITY. Section 338(h)(10) election creates $125-$143M of tax value ($117-$131M federal, $8-$12M state) through basis step-up without incremental seller tax cost. ASP should negotiate purchase price reduction of $82-$105M (capturing 70-80% of benefit based on superior bargaining position with distressed seller). Alternative: UEHI provides uncapped tax indemnity for Section 1060 allocation disputes if price reduction rejected. Appraisal engagement required at signing ($180-$320K cost, 45-60 day timeline). Probability of achieving benefit: 85-92% (high confidence given UEHI's tax-indifferent status from NOLs and established precedent for Section 338(h)(10) elections in distressed seller contexts).
